</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>Bioinformatics approaches have proven useful in understanding biological responses to spaceflight. Spaceflight experiments remain resource intensive and rare. One outstanding issue is how to maximize scientific output from a limited number of omics datasets from traditional animal models including nematodes, fruitfly, and rodents. The utility of omics data from invertebrate models in anticipating mammalian responses to spaceflight has not been fully explored. Hence, we performed comparative analyses of transcriptomes of soleus and extensor digitorum longus (EDL) in mice that underwent 37 days of spaceflight. Results indicate shared stress responses and altered circadian rhythm. EDL showed more robust growth signals and <em>Pde2a</em> downregulation, possibly underlying its resistance to atrophy versus soleus. Spaceflight and hindlimb unloading mice shared differential regulation of proliferation, circadian, and neuronal signaling. Shared gene regulation in muscles of humans on bedrest and space flown rodents suggest targets for mitigating muscle atrophy in space and on Earth. Spaceflight responses of <em>C. elegans</em> were more similar to EDL. Discrete life stages of <em>D. melanogaster</em> have distinct utility in anticipating EDL and soleus responses. In summary, spaceflight leads to shared and discrete molecular responses between muscle types and invertebrate models may augment mechanistic knowledge gained from rodent spaceflight and ground-based studies.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> spaceflight, muscle, transcriptomics, bedrest, hindlimb unloading, microgravity, animal models, cross-species comparison</p></section></section><section id="sec1-ijms-22-09470"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">1. Introduction</h2>
<p>Exposure to the unique set of spaceflight environmental conditions, including microgravity, cosmic radiation, isolation, and confinement, has been shown to induce significant physiological changes in all studied species to date [<a href="#B1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-ijms-22-09470">1</a>,<a href="#B2-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-ijms-22-09470">2</a>,<a href="#B3-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-ijms-22-09470">3</a>]. These physiological responses, such as skeletal muscle atrophy, can negatively impact the health of astronauts and may also compromise mission success if left unabated [<a href="#B4-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-ijms-22-09470">4</a>]. The precise molecular mechanisms underlying physiological deconditioning in spaceflight have yet to be fully described, and advancement of this mechanistic understanding is essential for the development of molecularly rationalized countermeasures [<a href="#B5-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5-ijms-22-09470">5</a>]. Such countermeasures can help maintain astronaut health during future spaceflight missions, including long duration voyages to Mars in which current countermeasures are likely to prove insufficient [<a href="#B6-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-ijms-22-09470">6</a>].</p>
<p>Studies leveraging data from omics disciplines, including transcriptomics, allow for a detailed study of the complex interactions between the molecular landscape of biological systems and spaceflight environmental conditions. Despite the scientific potential of omics datasets captured in spaceflight, only a relatively small number have been collected, mainly due to the resource-intensive nature of spaceflight experiments. NASA GeneLab was established to maximize the use of the limited number of spaceflight omics datasets. The datasets made publicly available via the GeneLab repository represent a variety of omics data types and a range of model organisms, including microbes, plants, cell cultures, vertebrates, and invertebrates [<a href="#B7-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B7-ijms-22-09470">7</a>,<a href="#B8-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-ijms-22-09470">8</a>]. Additionally, datasets from ground-based studies that are relevant to the spaceflight environment can be accessed via public repositories, including GeneLab and NCBI Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO) [<a href="#B9-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-ijms-22-09470">9</a>]. Although physiological and other functional data is required to establish clear relationships between omics changes and physiological changes, independent analysis of omics datasets is still useful for hypothesis generation, which can be used to inform future research directions. There remain numerous opportunities to use publicly available omics datasets to elucidate knowledge gaps in the field of space biology.</p>
<p>One such knowledge gap that currently requires further investigation is that of shared and discrete transcriptomic responses across biological tissue types, such as different muscles. Addressing this knowledge gap can prove useful for designing countermeasures that can target multiple tissues, or for providing evidence for the need to employ multiple countermeasures to provide comprehensive protection to a variety of tissues. Aside from its role in locomotion and posture, skeletal muscle plays other vital roles in whole-body health. Skeletal muscle physically shields organs and helps maintain temperature homeostasis. Additionally, as the body’s largest store of amino acids, skeletal muscle engages in crosstalk with other organs, to synthesize organ-specific proteins [<a href="#B10-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-ijms-22-09470">10</a>]. When the body is threatened by starvation or disease, skeletal muscle is broken down to provide other organs with energy [<a href="#B11-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11-ijms-22-09470">11</a>].</p>
<p>To date, antigravity muscles in the limbs have been the main focus for studying the skeletal muscle response to spaceflight. These muscles are key to maintaining posture under 1G conditions, and are thus highly susceptible to atrophy when unloaded [<a href="#B12-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B12-ijms-22-09470">12</a>]. Certain antigravity muscles in the lower limb, such as the soleus and gastrocnemius, are also important for circulation, as upon contraction they pump venous blood back to the heart, preventing pooling and stagnation [<a href="#B13-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13-ijms-22-09470">13</a>].</p>
<p>Alongside reduced muscle mass, strength, and endurance, it has been found that the mechanical unloading associated with microgravity induces a shift from type I slow twitch fibers to type II fast twitch fibers [<a href="#B14-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-ijms-22-09470">14</a>,<a href="#B15-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-ijms-22-09470">15</a>]. Previous spaceflight and ground studies in rodents and humans have also observed that antigravity muscles consisting of mainly type I (slow-twitch) fibers, which are designed for slow and sustained contraction, such as the soleus tend to be more susceptible to atrophy compared to antigravity muscles with more type II (fast-twitch) fibers [<a href="#B16-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-ijms-22-09470">16</a>]. Recently, Choi and colleagues reported findings from NASA’s Rodent Research-1 (RR-1) mission, in which mice were flown onboard the International Space Station (ISS) and exposed to microgravity for 37 days. They found that the weight of the soleus in space flown mice was reduced by ~19% compared to ground control mice [<a href="#B17-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-ijms-22-09470">17</a>]. In addition, they reported that the gastrocnemius, tibialis anterior (TA), extensor digitorum longus (EDL), and quadriceps did not show significant atrophy compared to ground control mice [<a href="#B17-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-ijms-22-09470">17</a>]. While significant atrophy of the soleus is consistent with findings from similar studies, the finding of non-significant atrophy in the gastrocnemius is contrary to previous space flown mouse studies [<a href="#B18-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-ijms-22-09470">18</a>,<a href="#B19-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19-ijms-22-09470">19</a>,<a href="#B20-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20-ijms-22-09470">20</a>].</p>
<p>Similarly, previous transcriptomic analyses of RR-1 data have reported that the soleus showed the greatest number of significant differentially expressed genes (DEGs) [<a href="#B21-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-ijms-22-09470">21</a>,<a href="#B22-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-ijms-22-09470">22</a>] compared to other muscle tissues. However, all muscle tissues did show significant gene expression changes, and hierarchical clustering of these changes indicated that the muscle tissues can be divided into two distinct groups. Group 1 consisted of plantar flexor muscles, soleus, and gastrocnemius, while group 2 consisted of dorsiflexors EDL and TA and knee extensors, quadriceps. In addition, microgravity responsive changes in immune-related pathway regulation appeared to be more prevalent in group 1 muscles compared to group 2 [<a href="#B21-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-ijms-22-09470">21</a>]. A different analysis of RR-1 data highlighted that changes in the expression of clock genes were fairly uniform across the muscle tissues, and yet distinct compared to other tissues such as the liver [<a href="#B22-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-ijms-22-09470">22</a>]. Taken together, these findings demonstrate that while certain muscles may not undergo significant atrophy in spaceflight, they still undergo molecular changes that may require intervention. Additionally, these findings support the hypothesis that in certain cases, the response to spaceflight is not identical between muscle tissues, due to their distinct morphologies and locomotive functions [<a href="#B16-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-ijms-22-09470">16</a>]. Further investigation is required to establish precise differences and similarities between the molecular responses of different muscle tissues. Hence in this study, we compared transcriptomic data from the fast-twitch enriched EDL, and the representative slow-twitch muscle soleus [<a href="#B23-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-ijms-22-09470">23</a>], collected during the RR-1 mission. We made use of two analysis approaches, Gene Set Enrichment Analysis (GSEA) and overrepresentation analysis (ORA) to determine the impact of the analysis approach on outcomes and to better anticipate the array of biological responses to spaceflight. Subsequently, to better understand the analogous nature of ground-based studies, transcriptomic data from the RR-1 quadriceps was compared to the vastus lateralis from a human bed rest study [<a href="#B24-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-ijms-22-09470">24</a>], and transcriptomic data from the gastrocnemius from a different space flown mouse study was compared to matching HU samples from that same study [<a href="#B25-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-ijms-22-09470">25</a>].</p>
<p>In addition to rodents, invertebrates, including <em>C. elegans</em> and <em>D. melanogaster</em>, have been established as important model organisms for understanding biological responses to the space environment [<a href="#B26-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-ijms-22-09470">26</a>]. While it is possible to isolate muscles in ground laboratories via dissection when the sample size of fresh animals is sufficient [<a href="#B27-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27-ijms-22-09470">27</a>], whole organism or composite structures such as the head are typically extracted for analysis in spaceflight experiments due to logistical challenges of dissecting samples in orbit. However, insights related to muscle can still be uncovered. For example, reproducible downregulation of muscle-related genes has been observed across the “International <em>C. elegans</em> Experiment FIRST” (ICE-FIRST) and “<em>C. elegans</em> RNA Interference in Space Experiment” (CERISE) microarray experiments [<a href="#B28-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-ijms-22-09470">28</a>,<a href="#B29-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-ijms-22-09470">29</a>]. Leandro and colleagues performed a comparative analysis of space flown <em>C. elegans</em> from the ICE-FIRST experiment and space flown <em>D. melanogaster</em> from the GENE experiment [<a href="#B3-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-ijms-22-09470">3</a>,<a href="#B30-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-ijms-22-09470">30</a>]. Three analysis approaches were tested on the small subset of orthologous genes between the two organisms. Only six genes were found to have a common transcriptomic response in both experiments, with these genes all downregulated and associated with metabolic and neuromuscular signaling [<a href="#B3-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-ijms-22-09470">3</a>,<a href="#B31-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-ijms-22-09470">31</a>].</p>
<p>Terrestrial studies have set a strong precedent for using invertebrate models to develop tools [<a href="#B13-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13-ijms-22-09470">13</a>,<a href="#B32-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-ijms-22-09470">32</a>,<a href="#B33-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33-ijms-22-09470">33</a>] and study conserved pathways in mammalian biological processes such as aging [<a href="#B34-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-ijms-22-09470">34</a>,<a href="#B35-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-ijms-22-09470">35</a>,<a href="#B36-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-ijms-22-09470">36</a>,<a href="#B37-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-ijms-22-09470">37</a>,<a href="#B38-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38-ijms-22-09470">38</a>]. However, the utility of omics data from space flown invertebrate studies, in anticipating aspects of mammalian responses to spaceflight, has yet to be fully investigated. With large, low cost cohorts of genetic mutants, interventional invertebrate studies are useful for establishing correlational relationships between molecular pathways and spaceflight phenotypes, with functional measures needed for causation [<a href="#B29-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-ijms-22-09470">29</a>]. We reason that if certain mechanisms are shared between mammals and invertebrate models, invertebrate datasets can potentially augment the limited quantity of vertebrate spaceflight omics datasets. This in turn may prove useful in anticipating mammalian responses to spaceflight. To test these concepts, we compared the abovementioned RR-1 EDL and soleus muscle datasets with transcriptomic datasets of <em>C. elegans</em> and <em>D. melanogaster</em> that were flown in the ICE-FIRST experiment and STS-121 mission, respectively. We found that spaceflight results in discrete transcriptomic signatures between representative slow and fast twitch muscles (soleus and EDL respectively), consistent with their opposing roles in flexion and differential sensitivities to unloading. In addition, transcriptomic analysis revealed that invertebrate models share select aspects of mammalian responses to spaceflight. The life stage of invertebrate models appears to impact the degree by which they recapitulate the responses of mammalian tissues to spaceflight.</p></section><section id="sec2-ijms-22-09470"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">2. Results</h2>
<section id="sec2dot1-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.1. Datasets Used in This Study</h3>
<p><a href="#ijms-22-09470-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a> shows the transcriptomic datasets used in this study. These datasets included RNA-seq and microarray datasets. Model organisms represented are mouse, fruitfly, and nematode. A bedrest study was also selected for analysis (Refer to Methods section for further details on criteria).</p>
<section class="tw xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-09470-t001"><h4 class="obj_head">Table 1.</h4>
<div class="caption p"><p>Datasets used in the study, including the organism’s name, duration of spaceflight or unloading, assay type, sex, strain, tissue, age, and sample size. Refer to GeneLab and GEO databases for further details. GC: ground control, FLT: spaceflight, VC: vivarium control, HU: hindlimb unloading, Gastroc: gastrocnemius, Quad: quadriceps, VL: vastus lateralis, F: female, M: male, Mx: mixed; H: hermaphrodite. Tg: Gal4-UAS transgenic line expressing two copies of eGFP from the hemolectin promoter. N: number of individuals, rep: replicate of pooled samples run for transcriptomic analysis. Refer to [<a href="#B24-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-ijms-22-09470">24</a>,<a href="#B25-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-ijms-22-09470">25</a>,<a href="#B37-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-ijms-22-09470">37</a>,<a href="#B38-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38-ijms-22-09470">38</a>,<a href="#B39-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B39-ijms-22-09470">39</a>,<a href="#B40-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40-ijms-22-09470">40</a>,<a href="#B41-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B41-ijms-22-09470">41</a>,<a href="#B42-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42-ijms-22-09470">42</a>,<a href="#B43-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-ijms-22-09470">43</a>] for links to GeneLab processed data and versions used in this study.</p></div>
<div class="tbl-box p" tabindex="0"><table class="content" frame="hsides" rules="groups">
<thead><tr>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-09470-t001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section><section id="sec2dot2-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.2. Comparative Analysis Reveals Key Differences in Space Flown Slow and Fast Twitch Muscle</h3>
<p>We first characterized the effects of spaceflight on fast and slow twitch muscle types (EDL and soleus, respectively) from the RR-1 Mission (<a href="#ijms-22-09470-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>). Differential expression analysis (DEA) of EDL and soleus from space flown mice compared with ground controls along with ORA and GSEA are found in <a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Tables S1–S6, Networks S1 and S2 and Figures S1 and S2</a>, respectively. Genes with the largest changes to their expression can provide insight on important biological processes that underlie spaceflight responses. Included in the top 10 upregulated genes in the EDL was the long non-coding RNA (<em>Lncbate10</em>) that has been shown to protect <em>Pgc1α</em> from repression [<a href="#B44-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-ijms-22-09470">44</a>], as well as <em>Mettl21e</em> which inhibits proteasomes [<a href="#B45-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45-ijms-22-09470">45</a>]. We also observed upregulation of the cell cycle-related gene <em>Cdk1</em> and <em>Mmp12</em>, which play a role in ECM remodeling. The top downregulated genes included <em>Pde2a</em>, a phosphodiesterase (PDE), which controls degradation of cAMP and cGMP; the downregulation of <em>Pde2a</em> suggests increased cAMP/cGMP signaling. Downregulated as well was <em>Lep</em>, involved in energy homeostasis, and <em>Pck1</em>, a regulator of gluconeogenesis. Additionally, we found downregulation of <em>Stum</em>, which functions in mechanotransduction. Enriched results for the EDL show a response to radiation and reactive oxygen species (ROS) and the induction of apoptosis. In addition, there was enrichment of inflammatory pathways including <em>Stat</em> and <em>Nf-</em><em>κb</em>, a response to wound healing and immune-related gene sets (GO:0045087, GO:0034097) involving the production of cytokines TGFβ, IL4, IL10, and type 1 interferon. Enrichment of genes related to glucocorticoid (GC) signaling, a stress response pathway, was also observed. We found overrepresentation of gene sets involved in the circadian rhythm (GO:0048511, GO:0007623) as reported previously [<a href="#B22-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-ijms-22-09470">22</a>,<a href="#B46-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46-ijms-22-09470">46</a>,<a href="#B47-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47-ijms-22-09470">47</a>,<a href="#B48-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B48-ijms-22-09470">48</a>], including downregulation of <em>Dbp</em>, a key circadian rhythm gene involved in inducing the transcription of other clock genes such as <em>mPer1</em> [<a href="#B49-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B49-ijms-22-09470">49</a>]. GSEA results also show enrichment of carbohydrate metabolism, consistent with previous studies reporting a greater reliance on glucose and a shift away from lipid metabolism [<a href="#B50-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50-ijms-22-09470">50</a>]. In addition, we found downregulation of lipid metabolism (GO:0006631) based on ORA of downregulated genes <em>Lep</em>, <em>Lpl</em>, and <em>Ppar</em><em>γ</em> [<a href="#B51-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-ijms-22-09470">51</a>,<a href="#B52-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52-ijms-22-09470">52</a>,<a href="#B53-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B53-ijms-22-09470">53</a>]. Analysis also revealed enrichment of lipid transport and sequestration in the EDL, consistent with previously reported lipid accumulation in atrophied muscles [<a href="#B50-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50-ijms-22-09470">50</a>]. ORA of upregulated DEGs in the EDL show proliferation-related gene sets (GO:0008283, GO:0000278) suggesting an increase in proliferation. Conversely, GSEA revealed enrichment of mitogenic signaling pathways (MAPK), which correspond to the upregulation of genes involved in cell cycle progression (<em>Cdk1</em>). GSEA also revealed regulation of proliferation and migration of vascular endothelial cells and angiogenesis (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Network 1, Supplementary Figure S1</a>), suggesting spaceflight-induced changes to muscle vasculature. Results also show the enrichment of muscle cell development, and differentiation as well as upregulation of <em>Fos</em>, <em>Mettl21e</em> and <em>Prnd</em>, genes involved in differentiation and the upregulation of <em>Pax3</em>, a stem cell marker expressed in activated satellite cells.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-09470-f001"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 1.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8430797_ijms-22-09470-g001.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/369c/8430797/c8ef34f9da86/ijms-22-09470-g001.jpg" loading="lazy" height="303" width="775" alt="Figure 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/ijms-22-09470-f001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Anatomical positions of the various mouse and human muscles used in this study, grouped by function. The right panel shows the percentage of slow and fast twitch fibers for each muscle.</p></figcaption></figure><p>In the soleus, the top upregulated genes consist of keratins, known to be important structural proteins in muscle as well as <em>Mettle21e</em>, involved in ECM remodeling, also found to be upregulated in the EDL. <em>Dhrs9</em>, involved in vitamin A biosynthesis, was downregulated, consistent with the observation of a reduction in retinol in lipid droplets in the liver of space flown mice [<a href="#B54-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B54-ijms-22-09470">54</a>]. Analyses also revealed downregulation of <em>Gcat</em>, which plays a role in threonine metabolism. Similarly, we found downregulation of <em>C9</em>, which functions in the innate immune response. Enriched results for the soleus suggest stress signaling with the enrichment of a response to GCs (GO:0051384) and the downregulation of <em>Ciart</em>, a circadian gene with a role in the transcriptional repression of <em>Clock</em> and <em>Bmal1</em> [<a href="#B55-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-ijms-22-09470">55</a>]. There were fewer immune related results in the soleus compared to the EDL (sole enrichment of interferon gamma and downregulation of <em>Il8</em> in the soleus). These results may suggest that the EDL has a more robust immune response than soleus, consistent with findings from a previous spaceflight study [<a href="#B56-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B56-ijms-22-09470">56</a>]. Further, GSEA indicated negative regulation of the unfolded protein response (UPR), suggesting downregulation of endoplasmic reticulum (ER) stress response in the soleus. Metabolic changes also differ in comparison to the EDL, with results indicating an increase in protein metabolism (GO:0006082, GO:0019752, GO:0072330) using upregulated genes in ORA. This is accompanied by negative correlation of ribosome biogenesis and negative nucleotide biosynthesis with spaceflight, all consistent with a decrease in muscle mass. While mixed results were reported for the regulation of lipid metabolism, the results suggest a decrease in glucose metabolism (GO:0005975). We also found enrichment of growth related gene sets such as upregulation of growth hormones including <em>Igf1</em> and its receptor, <em>Igf1r</em>. However, findings also included negative cell growth (GO:0016049) and proliferation (GO:0008283). The presence of positive and negative growth pathways may suggest remodeling of the muscles as they respond to the need to replenish and remove old or damaged cells in response to spaceflight. Similarly, results using up- and downregulated genes in ORA also indicate both positive and negative developmental cues including the enrichment of negative tissue development (GO:0009888) and cell differentiation (GO:0045595). A cross comparison of the biological processes shared between the EDL and soleus is shown in <a href="#ijms-22-09470-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>, and shared DEGs are presented in <a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S7</a>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-09470-f002"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 2.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8430797_ijms-22-09470-g002.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/369c/8430797/28407aacb954/ijms-22-09470-g002.jpg" loading="lazy" height="783" width="781" alt="Figure 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/ijms-22-09470-f002/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Shared upregulated and downregulated biological processes in soleus and EDL in response to spaceflight. The resulting gene ratio and <em>p</em> values from Webgestalt analysis of DEGs are shown.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec2dot3-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.3. Molecular Signatures of <em>C. elegans</em> in Comparison with Fast and Slow Twitch Muscles during Spaceflight</h3>
<section id="sec2dot3dot1-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.3.1. Shared Increase in Proliferation between Space Flown <em>C. Elegans</em> and Mouse Fast Twitch EDL</h4>
<p>We next asked whether invertebrate models can be used to gain insight into mammalian responses to spaceflight. To address this question, we performed ORA on space flown <em>C. elegans</em> and utilized Ensembl orthology [<a href="#B57-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B57-ijms-22-09470">57</a>] to perform a cross-species comparison with mouse muscles to assess whether the transcriptional responses to spaceflight found in mammalian muscle are also observed in nematodes. DEGs and ORA can be found in <a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Tables S8 and S9</a>. Differential expression analysis of space flown <em>C. elegans</em> revealed 106 DEGs (FDR &lt; 0.05). Initial inspection of the enriched biological processes of <em>C. elegans</em> revealed that most upregulated biological processes (BP) are related to cell division (GO:0007049, GO:0051301) and development (GO:0032502), processes akin to the phenotype defined in the EDL. We also found upregulation of genes related to cytoskeleton organization (GO:0000226, GO:0007010), chitin metabolism (GO:0006030), and downregulation of ECM organization (GO:0030198). A cross-comparison between <em>C. elegans</em> and EDL enriched biological processes, shown in <a href="#ijms-22-09470-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>, also indicates the shared overrepresentation of proliferation-related gene sets (GO:0008283, GO:0000278, GO:0022402). Gene sets related to the immune response (GO:0006955), synaptic signaling (GO:0098916) and the ECM (GO:0030198) were upregulated in the EDL but downregulated in <em>C. elegans.</em> A comparison of <em>C. elegans</em> and EDL DEGs revealed seven shared genes. These include an oncogene with a role in cell polarity (<em>Cab39</em>) [<a href="#B58-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58-ijms-22-09470">58</a>] and a gene involved in base excision repair (<em>Ung</em>) [<a href="#B59-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B59-ijms-22-09470">59</a>]. An early development gene <em>Zcchc24</em> [<a href="#B60-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B60-ijms-22-09470">60</a>] was downregulated in both datasets. In addition, we found downregulation of <em>Rab20</em>, a gene involved in cell trafficking in the Golgi apparatus [<a href="#B61-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B61-ijms-22-09470">61</a>], consistent with downregulation of cell-cell signaling (GO:0007267). Genes upregulated in <em>C. elegans</em> but downregulated in the EDL have roles in uracil methylation (<em>Trmt44</em>), calcium-mediated synaptic transmission (<em>Syt12</em>), and thrombin degradation (<em>Thbd</em>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-09470-f003"><h5 class="obj_head">Figure 3.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8430797_ijms-22-09470-g003.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/369c/8430797/c69d99e83b28/ijms-22-09470-g003.jpg" loading="lazy" height="723" width="790" alt="Figure 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/ijms-22-09470-f003/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Shared upregulated and downregulated biological processes in <em>C. elegans</em> compared with soleus and the EDL in response to spaceflight. The resulting gene ratio and P values from Webgestalt analysis of DEGs are shown.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec2dot3dot2-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.3.2. Comparison between <em>C. elegans</em> and Mouse Slow Twitch Muscle, Soleus</h4>
<p>Similar to findings from comparisons with EDL, we also found shared biological processes between <em>C. elegans</em> and soleus (<a href="#ijms-22-09470-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>), namely downregulation of cell proliferation. However, other processes were regulated in opposite directions. Specifically, transmembrane transport (GO:0055085), defense response (GO:0006952, GO:0098542), and cell adhesion (GO:0007155) were downregulated in C. elegans, yet upregulated in the soleus. We found 16 shared DEGs in <em>C. elegans</em> and soleus (<a href="#ijms-22-09470-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). Upregulated in both <em>C. elegans</em> and soleus are mitochondrial genes involved in mtDNA repair (Mpv17), branched chain amino acid metabolism (<em>Ppm1k</em>) [<a href="#B62-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B62-ijms-22-09470">62</a>], and the DNA helicase gene <em>Mcm2</em>. Shared downregulated genes include Thbd, involved in thrombin degradation as well as Rab20, previously described. Genes that were upregulated in <em>C. elegans</em> but downregulated in the soleus include those involved in DNA repair (<em>Nsmce1</em>) [<a href="#B63-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B63-ijms-22-09470">63</a>], and the unfolded protein response (<em>Shq1</em>), suggesting increased damage response in C. elegans. Genes that are downregulated in <em>C. elegans</em> but upregulated in the soleus are involved in muscle excitability (<em>Ric3</em>) [<a href="#B64-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-ijms-22-09470">64</a>] and synaptic signaling (<em>Syt12</em>, <em>Ric3</em>, <em>Htr7</em>) [<a href="#B64-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-ijms-22-09470">64</a>,<a href="#B65-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B65-ijms-22-09470">65</a>,<a href="#B66-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-ijms-22-09470">66</a>] which may impact muscle contraction if globally upregulated in neurons. Downregulated in <em>C. elegans</em> were <em>Dpp4</em>, a gene encoding a multi-functional transmembrane protein involved in glucose uptake [<a href="#B67-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67-ijms-22-09470">67</a>], and <em>Plce1</em> involved in growth and differentiation [<a href="#B68-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B68-ijms-22-09470">68</a>]. <em>Pde4b</em>, which codes for an enzyme that degrades cAMP to AMP, was downregulated, suggesting increased cAMP signaling in <em>C. elegans</em>, which is more akin to the EDL response.</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-09470-t002/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section></section><section id="sec2dot4-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4. Cross-Comparison between Space Flown D. melanogaster and Mouse Muscles</h3>
<section id="sec2dot4dot1-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4.1. Shared Stress Response in EDL and Larval <em>D. melanogaster</em>
</h4>
<p>We also determined whether space flown <em>D. melanogaster</em> share any similarities with mammalian muscle responses spaceflight. To determine this, we individually compared EDL with larvae and adult <em>D. melanogaster.</em> We reasoned that doing such may provide insight into any life-stage dependencies in our findings. DEA of space flown <em>D. melanogaster</em> larvae revealed 439 orthologous mouse genes (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S10</a>), which were subjected to ORA (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S11</a>). Genes were compared with mouse EDL, revealing 18 shared DEGs shown in <a href="#ijms-22-09470-t003" class="usa-link">Table 3</a>. Shared upregulated genes include the circadian rhythm gene <em>N</em><em>oct</em>, consistent with previous observations in space flown <em>D. melanogaster</em> [<a href="#B69-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69-ijms-22-09470">69</a>]. Stress-induced molecular chaperones (<em>Hsp90aa1</em>) were also upregulated as well as genes involved in the biosynthesis of carnitine and fatty acid transport <em>(Tmlhe</em>). Shared downregulated genes include <em>Mettl26,</em> a methyltransferase. Genes with opposing patterns of regulation reveal key differences in metabolism including <em>D2hgdh</em>, upregulated in <em>D. melanogaster</em>, involved in D2-HG metabolism in the mitochondria, and <em>Cth</em>, involved in cysteine biosynthesis. Genes that were upregulated in the EDL but downregulated in <em>D. melanogaster</em> suggest differential regulation of glycosylation (<em>Gmppb</em>), the cytoskeleton (<em>Tubb4b</em>) and ECM organization (<em>Hspg2</em>, <em>Prcp</em>), blood pressure (<em>Prcp)</em>, and protein ubiquitination <em>(Plaa</em>).</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-09470-t003/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section><section id="sec2dot4dot2-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4.2. Shared Responses of Larval <em>D. melanogaster</em> and Soleus from Space Flown Mice</h4>
<p>A comparison of larval <em>D. melanogaster</em> DEGs revealed 61 shared genes with the soleus (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S12</a>). ORA of the 38 upregulated orthologs (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S13</a>) suggested a shared increase in protein metabolism (GO:0006520, GO:0000096, GO:0006534). Notably, both datasets include the downregulation of <em>Pdia6</em>, which promotes proliferation [<a href="#B70-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B70-ijms-22-09470">70</a>,<a href="#B71-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B71-ijms-22-09470">71</a>] and a mitochondrial gene, <em>Gtpbp3</em>, for which studies involving its knockdown reported reduced ATP generation, increased ROS, and apoptosis [<a href="#B72-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B72-ijms-22-09470">72</a>]. The molecular chaperones <em>Hspa5</em> and <em>Hspb6</em> were also downregulated, suggesting a reduction of UPR signaling and consistent with increased protein metabolism, features observed in the soleus. <em>Scarb1</em> was downregulated in both soleus and <em>D. melanogaster</em> larvae. SCARB1 has been shown to play an important role in muscle regeneration [<a href="#B73-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B73-ijms-22-09470">73</a>]. Similarly, <em>Crym</em> was downregulated, knockout of which in mice leads to hypertrophy of fast glycolytic fibers [<a href="#B74-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B74-ijms-22-09470">74</a>]. <em>Pnf4</em>, important for actin polymerization [<a href="#B75-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B75-ijms-22-09470">75</a>] was also downregulated in both soleus and <em>D. melanogaster</em> larvae.</p></section><section id="sec2dot4dot3-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4.3. Comparison of Space Flown Adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> and Fast Twitch Mammalian Muscles</h4>
<p>We next compared adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> to the mouse muscles. Spaceflight led to 459 DEGs (FDR&lt;0.05) in adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S14</a>). Of these, we found 101 that are orthologous to mouse genes, which were used for ORA (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S15</a>). Four of the DEGs were found to be shared with the mouse EDL dataset. Genes that showed upregulation of expression in the two datasets were found to be involved in chromatin scaffolding (<em>Ppp1r10)</em> and replication (<em>Mcm6</em>). <em>Ppm1l</em>, downregulated in both datasets, is a negative regulator of stress and inflammatory cytokines. Hence, its downregulation in the EDL is consistent with an increased immune response [<a href="#B76-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B76-ijms-22-09470">76</a>]. Also dysregulated was <em>Hspg2,</em> which encodes an ECM protein.</p></section><section id="sec2dot4dot4-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4.4. Comparison of Space Flown Adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> with Mouse Soleus</h4>
<p>A comparison of adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> DEGs with soleus DEGs (FDR &lt; 0.1) revealed 12 shared genes shown in <a href="#ijms-22-09470-t004" class="usa-link">Table 4</a>. Genes that are upregulated in both datasets include a myosin protein ligase (<em>Mylip</em>) involved in muscle atrophy, previously reported to be induced by glucocorticoids [<a href="#B77-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B77-ijms-22-09470">77</a>]. Also upregulated were genes encoding a helicase protein <em>Mcm2</em> involved in replication and the pro-survival gene, <em>Ppm11</em>, which negatively regulates SAPK-mediated apoptosis. However, <em>Jarid2</em> was also upregulated in both, which represses pro-cell cycle genes [<a href="#B78-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B78-ijms-22-09470">78</a>]. Additionally, <em>Cbs</em> which takes part in the transulfuration pathways in cysteine production, is also upregulated. Genes that are downregulated in both datasets encode a nucleoporin (<em>Nup37</em>), a polymerase (<em>Polr2e</em>) and <em>Dnajb4</em>, the suppression of which has been linked to decreased growth [<a href="#B79-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79-ijms-22-09470">79</a>]. Genes upregulated in <em>D. melanogaster</em> but downregulated in mouse soleus include the helicase subunit (<em>Mcm6</em>) and genes involved in cysteine production (<em>Gnmt</em>), calcium signaling (<em>Inpp5a</em>), and limb development (<em>Fjx1</em>).</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-09470-t004/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section></section><section id="sec2dot5-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.5. Comparison between Ground-Based Unloading Models and Muscles from Space Flown Mice</h3>
<section id="sec2dot5dot1-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.5.1. Comparison between Gastrocnemius from Hindlimb Unloaded and Space Flown Mice</h4>
<p>To gain insight on shared transcriptomic signatures of ground-based unloading models and space flown mice, a transcriptomic analysis was conducted on RNA of gastrocnemius from space flown mice and a time and age-matched HU study (GLDS-21). Analyses revealed 75 DEGs (<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) in the gastrocnemius from hindlimb unloading (HU) mice (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S16</a>) and 115 DEGs (<em>p</em> &lt; 0.05) in the gastrocnemius from space flown mice (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S17</a>). The top upregulated genes in the HU model have roles in UPR (<em>Hspa1b</em>, <em>Hspa1a</em>, <em>Atsf3</em>), and fatty acid biosynthesis and metabolism (<em>Fasn</em>, <em>Ppargc1a</em>). Proto-oncogenes <em>Fos</em> and <em>Jun</em> were also upregulated in HU mice. In contrast, a previous study reported upregulation of <em>Fos</em> and <em>Jun</em> in human muscle after exercise [<a href="#B80-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B80-ijms-22-09470">80</a>]. Additionally, the top 10 most downregulated genes include mitochondrial genes such as <em>Idh2</em>, which takes part in the electron transport chain, <em>Bdh1</em> involved in ketone metabolism, and <em>Ldhb</em>, which catalyzes the conversion of pyruvate to lactate. ORA performed on DEGs from gastrocnemius of HU mice (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S18</a>) revealed differential regulation of stress responses (GO:0033554, GO:0009628), as well as muscle tissue proliferation (GO:0048659), differentiation (GO:0035914) and development (GO:0007519).</p>
<p>Conversely, the top upregulated genes in the gastrocnemius from space flown mice include <em>Mt1</em> and <em>Mt2</em>, genes that have antioxidant activity and glucocorticoid response elements [<a href="#B81-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81-ijms-22-09470">81</a>]. We also found upregulation of a number of tumor suppressor genes such as <em>Btg2</em>, <em>Cebpd</em>, <em>Gadd45g</em>, <em>Cdkn1a</em> and <em>Tp53inp1</em> [<a href="#B82-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B82-ijms-22-09470">82</a>,<a href="#B83-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B83-ijms-22-09470">83</a>,<a href="#B84-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B84-ijms-22-09470">84</a>,<a href="#B85-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B85-ijms-22-09470">85</a>,<a href="#B86-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B86-ijms-22-09470">86</a>], suggesting reduced growth and proliferation. <em>Cidec</em>, which codes for a protein that promotes lipid droplet formation in adipocytes [<a href="#B87-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B87-ijms-22-09470">87</a>], was also upregulated. ORA of upregulated DEGs in spaceflight gastrocnemius (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S18</a>) suggests a stress response as shown by differential regulation of GC signaling (GO:0071385). ORA also revealed upregulation of glucose metabolism (GO:0032868) in the gastrocnemius from space flown mice, suggesting alterations in energy homeostasis.</p>
<p>A comparison of DEGs in HU and spaceflight gastrocnemius revealed five shared genes with similar patterns of regulation. DEGs upregulated in both datasets suggest that HU and spaceflight both lead to altered circadian rhythm (<em>Nfil3</em>), regulation of proliferation (<em>Btg2</em>) and changes to endothelial adhesion (<em>Cyr61</em>), and scaffolding of acetylcholine receptors at the neuromuscular junction (<em>Musk</em>), which may potentially alter muscle function. Additionally, <em>Bdh1</em>, a gene involved in metabolism of ketone bodies, is downregulated in both datasets. This is consistent with a previous study showing an increase in ketone bodies such as 3-hydroxybutyrate in liver of mice flown on STS-135 [<a href="#B54-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B54-ijms-22-09470">54</a>].</p></section><section id="sec2dot5dot2-ijms-22-09470"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.5.2. Comparison of Muscles from Bed Rest Study and Space Flown Mice</h4>
<p>Next, we investigated shared transcriptomic responses between representative mixed fiber type muscles, and vastus lateralis muscles (VL) obtained from a bedrest study (NCBI GEO <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE24215" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">GSE24215</a>/GLDS-370) (349 DEGs, ± 1.5 FC, <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05, <a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S19</a>) versus quadriceps of space flown mice (887 DEGs, <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05), <a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S20</a>). Eight genes were shared between these datasets (<a href="#ijms-22-09470-t005" class="usa-link">Table 5</a>). Upregulated in both datasets was <em>Tbc1d12</em> involved in increasing glucose uptake [<a href="#B88-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B88-ijms-22-09470">88</a>], whereas the shared downregulated genes included <em>Ptp4a3</em>, the downregulation of which increases expression of ECM genes [<a href="#B89-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-ijms-22-09470">89</a>], and <em>Fbxo40</em>, the downregulation of which has been observed in muscular dystrophy [<a href="#B90-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B90-ijms-22-09470">90</a>]. Additionally, <em>Ssmpx</em>, a gene induced by stretching of muscles, was downregulated in the human VL but upregulated in mouse quadriceps [<a href="#B91-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B91-ijms-22-09470">91</a>]. ORA of DEGs in human VL (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S21</a>) revealed downregulation of nucleotide metabolism (GO:0009117, GO:0006753) and oxidation-reduction processes usually associated with oxidative phosphorylation (GO:0055114). Conversely, ORA results for mouse quadriceps (<a href="#app1-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link">Supplementary Table S22</a>) showed downregulation of protein metabolism (GO:0030163, GO:0044257, GO:0051603, and GO:0006511) and a response to stress (GO:0033554).</p>
