<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-09470-t005/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section></section></section></section><section id="sec3-ijms-22-09470"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">3. Discussion</h2>
<section id="sec3dot1-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.1. Mechanisms of Muscle Atrophy Resistance</h3>
<p>Skeletal muscles are highly adaptive to changes in mechanical forces and display a robust regenerative capacity. In response to weight-bearing or cell damage, cells can initiate pro-survival signaling pathways that increase cell viability and growth, maintain cell number homeostasis, and allow proliferation to meet functional needs. Analysis of the transcriptomic response in the EDL from space flown animals reveals enrichment and overrepresentation of gene sets involved in necrosis, wound healing, immune response, and proliferation, which altogether may suggest a compensatory proliferation in reaction to cell damage. Since mature muscles cells do not divide, the enrichment of proliferative terms may represent the recruitment of satellite cells, which then differentiate into new myofibers [<a href="#B92-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B92-ijms-22-09470">92</a>] to achieve muscle regeneration after cellular insult [<a href="#B93-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93-ijms-22-09470">93</a>]. This hypothesis is supported by enrichment of developmental GO terms and the upregulation of genes such as <em>Fos</em> and <em>Pax3</em> known to be expressed in activated satellite cells during muscle cell development and in early muscle regeneration post trauma [<a href="#B94-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B94-ijms-22-09470">94</a>,<a href="#B95-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B95-ijms-22-09470">95</a>]. Previous studies also show tissue regeneration markers of satellite cell activation in murine quadriceps during spaceflight [<a href="#B96-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96-ijms-22-09470">96</a>]. Spaceflight and its analogs can lead to impaired immunity [<a href="#B97-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97-ijms-22-09470">97</a>,<a href="#B98-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98-ijms-22-09470">98</a>,<a href="#B99-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99-ijms-22-09470">99</a>,<a href="#B100-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B100-ijms-22-09470">100</a>,<a href="#B101-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101-ijms-22-09470">101</a>,<a href="#B102-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B102-ijms-22-09470">102</a>]. A more robust immune response in the EDL may also indicate damage and repair events in this muscle, given the role the immune system plays in the regeneration process [<a href="#B103-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103-ijms-22-09470">103</a>,<a href="#B104-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B104-ijms-22-09470">104</a>]. In the EDL, the upregulation of <em>Il15</em>, which codes for an immune modulating cytokine previously noted to confer hypertrophic effects. Increased <em>Il15</em> expression may contribute to regeneration and prevent excessive atrophy in response to unloading [<a href="#B105-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B105-ijms-22-09470">105</a>]. Additionally, these results also suggest changes to vasculature, which may impact nutrient availability and therefore growth. Previous work on the HU model revealed impaired vasodilation [<a href="#B106-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B106-ijms-22-09470">106</a>] and vasoconstriction [<a href="#B107-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B107-ijms-22-09470">107</a>] in feed arteries of weight bearing plantar flexors. Similarly, radiation exposure has been shown to impact development in human vessel models [<a href="#B108-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B108-ijms-22-09470">108</a>]. The involvement of the above mentioned differentially expressed genes in skeletal muscle atrophy can be tested in future spaceflight experiments.</p>
<p>A genetic mechanism that may contribute to the difference in sensitivity to atrophy between the EDL and soleus may involve regulation of PDEs, which hydrolyze and tightly control cAMP/cGMP. cAMP/cGMP are ubiquitous second messenger signaling molecules known to protect against atrophy, increase myofiber size, and promote conversion to faster glycolytic fibers [<a href="#B109-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B109-ijms-22-09470">109</a>]. Our results showed downregulation of phosphodiesterase genes (<em>Pde2a)</em> in the EDL suggesting increased cAMP/gAMP signaling. In contrast, the upregulation of <em>Pde2a</em>, along with five other phosphodiesterase genes (<em>Pde3a</em>, <em>Pde4a</em>, <em>Pde4b</em>, <em>Pde4c</em>, <em>Pde9a</em>) in the soleus suggests decreased cAMP/cGMP signaling. Further work is needed to define levels of cAMP signaling in different muscle types during spaceflight and whether this pathway can be exploited to prevent muscle atrophy during spaceflight. Additionally, in the EDL, a long non-coding RNA (<em>Lncbate10)</em> was upregulated, which is known to protect <em>Pgc1α</em> from repression in adipose tissues [<a href="#B44-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-ijms-22-09470">44</a>]. This coincides with the upregulation of <em>Pgc1α</em>, a gene whose expression is also known to protect against atrophy [<a href="#B110-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B110-ijms-22-09470">110</a>]. The expression of <em>Lncbate10</em> has been shown to be induced by high cAMP concentrations, consistent with the downregulation of the cAMP regulator, <em>Pde2a</em> [<a href="#B44-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-ijms-22-09470">44</a>]. Spaceflight is known to induce slow-to-fast muscle fiber conversion [<a href="#B111-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B111-ijms-22-09470">111</a>]. In addition to <em>Pgc1α</em> upregulation in the EDL, we also observed the upregulation of <em>Mettl21e</em>, both of which are known to drive hypertrophy of type II myofibers [<a href="#B45-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45-ijms-22-09470">45</a>,<a href="#B112-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B112-ijms-22-09470">112</a>]. Additionally, we observed the downregulation of <em>Vegfa</em> in the soleus, which was not observed in the EDL. This suggests opposing regulation of a key growth signal in vasculature of the two muscle types, which influence nutrient availability and ultimately, the propensity for muscle growth.</p></section><section id="sec3dot2-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.2. Glucocorticoids and the Circadian Rhythm</h3>
<p>The enrichment of glucocorticoid (GC) signaling suggests that spaceflight led to upregulation of the stress response. This is consistent with the reported rise in plasma and urine cortisol levels observed in certain but not all of spaceflight studies [<a href="#B113-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B113-ijms-22-09470">113</a>,<a href="#B114-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B114-ijms-22-09470">114</a>]. GCs are steroidal signaling molecules with many roles including the regulation of energy homeostasis. They can cause the breakdown of amino acids and a decrease in insulin sensitivity, which were previously observed in the soleus [<a href="#B115-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115-ijms-22-09470">115</a>]. GCs can also induce expression of clock genes via GC response elements and may therefore have a role in perturbing the circadian rhythm and sleep cycles during spaceflight [<a href="#B22-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-ijms-22-09470">22</a>,<a href="#B116-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B116-ijms-22-09470">116</a>,<a href="#B117-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B117-ijms-22-09470">117</a>,<a href="#B118-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B118-ijms-22-09470">118</a>]. The disturbance of the circadian rhythm in both muscles (EDL and soleus) may also negatively impact muscle mass. For example, knockout of a key circadian gene, <em>Bmal</em> in mice led to premature aging including sarcopenia [<a href="#B119-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B119-ijms-22-09470">119</a>]. Perturbations in GC and the circadian signaling have been implicated in disorders such as metabolic syndrome, [<a href="#B120-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B120-ijms-22-09470">120</a>,<a href="#B121-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B121-ijms-22-09470">121</a>,<a href="#B122-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B122-ijms-22-09470">122</a>], sleep disturbances [<a href="#B123-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B123-ijms-22-09470">123</a>,<a href="#B124-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B124-ijms-22-09470">124</a>], immune dysregulation [<a href="#B100-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B100-ijms-22-09470">100</a>,<a href="#B125-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B125-ijms-22-09470">125</a>,<a href="#B126-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B126-ijms-22-09470">126</a>] and cataracts [<a href="#B127-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B127-ijms-22-09470">127</a>]. Hence, these signaling pathways may potentially represent therapeutic targets for the detrimental effects of spaceflight.</p></section><section id="sec3dot3-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.3. Spaceflight Alters Mechanosensing and Neuronal Signaling in Mouse Muscle</h3>
<p>Transcriptomic analysis of muscles from space flown mice revealed alterations in signaling pathways involved in mechanotransduction and neuromuscular communication. For example, results showed downregulation of <em>Stum</em> in the EDL, which encodes a protein important for the sensing of mechanical stimuli in proprioceptive neurons [<a href="#B128-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B128-ijms-22-09470">128</a>]. This finding is consistent with the effects of unloading and reduced proprioception observed during spaceflight [<a href="#B128-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B128-ijms-22-09470">128</a>,<a href="#B129-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B129-ijms-22-09470">129</a>]. Similarly, we observed enrichment of genes involved in Rho signaling, one of the pathways that can promote stress fiber formation [<a href="#B130-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B130-ijms-22-09470">130</a>]. Contractility changes in stress fibers is thought to be one the mechanisms by which mechanical forces exerted on the extracellular matrix (ECM) can be sensed by a cell [<a href="#B131-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B131-ijms-22-09470">131</a>]. We also found differential expression of a number of genes involved in neuron excitability such as <em>Grin2b,</em> the dysregulation of which leads to decreased muscle tone [<a href="#B132-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B132-ijms-22-09470">132</a>]. In the soleus, the enrichment of gene sets involved in nervous system development and negative axon extension and the downregulation of neurogenesis (GO:0022008) suggest changes to motor neuron signaling.</p></section><section id="sec3dot4-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.4. <em>C. elegans</em> Shows Similarity to Mouse Fast Twitch Muscle Responses to Spaceflight</h3>
<p>Rodents have been widely used to extrapolate human responses to spaceflight due to their significant genetic, physiological, and anatomical similarities. <em>C. elegans</em>, however, have been widely used to study developmental processes since at least 83% of the proteome has a human homologue and ~8000 of its proteins have matching human gene transcripts [<a href="#B133-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B133-ijms-22-09470">133</a>]. The use of <em>C. elegans</em> as a spaceflight model has advantages over rodent models such as their relatively simpler maintenance requirements and smaller body size that allows for a larger number of individuals to be flown in space. In addition, <em>C. elegans</em> allows the use of genetic and molecular tools to dissect specific pathways and can be used for the analysis of potential countermeasures. A caveat of our comparison is that the transcriptomics was performed on a population of animals of mixed ages, and that nematodes contain multiple tissues—neurons, gut, reproductive tissue, in addition to muscles. Understanding how responses in <em>C. elegans</em> mirror mammalian responses to spaceflight may allow for better extrapolation to mammalian results. While the comparison between <em>C. elegans</em> and mouse muscles revealed differences in spaceflight-induced regulation of developmental genes, there was a shared increase in proliferation-related terms with the more atrophy resistant EDL. Similarly, we posit that <em>C. elegans</em> may resist muscle atrophy through shared increased cAMP signaling as indicated by the downregulation of <em>Pde4b</em>, encoding a cAMP regulatory enzyme, variants of which were upregulated in the soleus. The more proliferative transcriptomic signature in <em>C. elegans</em> is more similar to that of the EDL, suggesting shared mechanisms of resistance and may point to a utility for the organism in anticipating responses of fast twitch muscle fibers. Additionally, while <em>C. elegans</em> have been used for uncovering mechanisms of innate immunity analogous to humans, their immune response to spaceflight was not comparable to that seen in fast or slow twitch muscle. This may be due to the lack of an adaptive immune system in <em>C. elegans</em> [<a href="#B134-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B134-ijms-22-09470">134</a>].</p></section><section id="sec3dot5-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.5. Discrete Life Stages of D. melanogaster May Have Distinct Utility for Studying Muscle Types</h3>
<p>Establishing shared responses to spaceflight between <em>D. melanogaster</em> and mammalian muscles also may be of value in anticipating mammalian responses to spaceflight. <em>D. melanogaster</em> are desirable as flight payloads due to their relatively small experimental footprint and their ease of maintenance. A comparison between different life stages of <em>D. melanogaster</em> and mammalian fast and slow twitch muscles revealed that discrete fly life stages may have differing utility for modeling certain features of the response to spaceflight in these mammalian muscle types. For example, an increase in protein metabolism as well as the downregulation of the UPR, two key features of the soleus response, were observed in <em>D. melanogaster</em> larvae. Conversely, protein metabolism was downregulated in adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> and not observed in the fast twitch EDL. This may suggest stage-dependent differences in metabolism. Moreover, other features of mature adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> are more akin to the fast twitch muscle, including an increase in amino acid production, calcium signaling and limb morphogenesis suggesting an environment with more growth signals. With regards to metabolism, the increase in expression of genes involved in fatty acid transport in adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> is akin to that of the EDL. However, adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> also shows a shared degradation of myosin protein with slow twitch muscle EDL. Furthermore, we also found a similar stress response between space flown adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> and mouse fast twitch muscle as indicated by differential regulation of circadian rhythm and antioxidant response genes.</p>
<p>Spaceflight and analog studies using <em>D. melanogaster</em> may allow for greater mechanistic insight on muscle gene regulation under spaceflight conditions. To date, on-orbit sampling of invertebrate models has mostly involved whole animals due to the logistical challenges of performing microdissections of tissues. Transcriptomic signatures can vary across muscle types and such differences may not be captured when analyzing the overall transcriptome from the whole organism. For example, each muscle type in <em>D. melanogaster</em> has a discrete pattern of isoform expression of Troponin, which regulates thin filament contraction [<a href="#B30-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-ijms-22-09470">30</a>,<a href="#B135-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B135-ijms-22-09470">135</a>]. We found that these isoforms are also differentially expressed in both spaceflight and analog experiments, consistent with a previous report [<a href="#B30-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-ijms-22-09470">30</a>]. Advances in sample preservation and in situ dissections on orbit will greatly improve our ability to gain mechanistic knowledge from smaller model organisms.</p></section><section id="sec3dot6-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.6. Differential Regulation of ECM in Spaceflight across Organismal Models</h3>
<p>Our results indicate differential regulation of ECM-related processes in <em>C. elegans</em>, <em>D. melanogaster</em>, and rodent EDL in response to spaceflight. Specifically, <em>C. elegans</em> showed downregulation of ECM organization and cell adhesion gene sets while these were upregulated in the EDL. Similarly, there was downregulation of ECM genes <em>Hspg2</em>, <em>Prcp</em> in larval <em>D. melanogaster</em>, while these genes were upregulated in the EDL. <em>Hspg2</em> encodes Perlecan, which is involved in ECM organization [<a href="#B135-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B135-ijms-22-09470">135</a>]. PRCP activates Prekallikrein, which cleaves fibronectin, a component of the ECM [<a href="#B136-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B136-ijms-22-09470">136</a>]. <em>Hspg2</em> was also differentially regulated in adult <em>D. melanogaster</em>. Comparison of vastus lateralis from bedrest and quadriceps from space flown mice also revealed a shared upregulation of <em>Ptp4a3</em>, the downregulation of which increases expression of ECM genes [<a href="#B89-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89-ijms-22-09470">89</a>]. These results suggest a general trend of increased expression of ECM-related genes in rodents and humans compared to downregulation in smaller model organisms such as <em>D. melanogaster</em> and <em>C. elegans</em>. The ECM plays important roles in a plethora of biological processes ranging from growth, migration, structural organization, barrier formation, and the immune response [<a href="#B137-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B137-ijms-22-09470">137</a>]. It is also impacted by spaceflight or simulated microgravity, as part of the mechanotransduction pathways involving integrins and mechanically activated ion channels [<a href="#B138-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B138-ijms-22-09470">138</a>]. The differences in the direction of regulation of ECM-related genes may be due to differences in body or tissue structure (hence differing ECM composition) and the differences in the timing of sampling and duration of flight. However, differential expression of ECM-related genes in all spaceflight models and analogs tested in this study suggest that ECM remodeling is likely to be a universal response to spaceflight.</p></section><section id="sec3dot7-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">3.7. Transcriptomic Signatures of Spaceflight Models and Analogs Exhibit Similarities and Key Differences</h3>
<p>Ground-based analogs such as HU have proven useful in anticipating many aspects of the musculoskeletal response to microgravity. Analysis of the transcriptome of the gastrocnemius from space flown rodents and a time-matched HU study revealed differences in transcriptomic signatures between these two models, particularly in the direction of regulation of genes involved in cell proliferation, circadian rhythm, endothelial cell adhesion, and motor neuron signaling. These changes are consistent with musculoskeletal atrophy, gait changes, sleep disturbances, and altered vascular function in humans that experienced spaceflight. However, we recognize that experimental limitations may impact comparative analysis of ground-based and spaceflight studies in rodents. Specifically, differences in animal handling and housing of space and ground-based rodent models can potentially affect the results. The use of appropriate housing controls and introduction of manipulations in controls that consider the additional handling associated with sending payloads to space (e.g., transport in ground vehicles, landing forces, light-dark cycle changes) may minimize the confounding factors when comparing findings from ground-based versus spaceflight rodent studies.</p>
<p>We also compared muscles from a rodent spaceflight and human bedrest study, two of the relatively resource intensive approaches to modeling human responses to spaceflight. Both have key advantages relative to ground-based rodent models. The former is expected to reflect mammalian responses to actual spaceflight and the later, actual human physiological responses to unloading. We found that representative mixed fiber type muscles in HU mice and humans on bedrest (quadriceps and VL, respectively) share similarities in differential expression of <em>Tbc1d12</em>, a gene involved in glucose uptake. In addition, <em>Fbxo40</em>, a gene that is also upregulated in a denervation model for muscle atrophy [<a href="#B90-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B90-ijms-22-09470">90</a>] is similarly responsive to both spaceflight in rodents and bedrest. Further, FBXO40 functions as a muscle-specific E3 ubiquitin ligase that is regulated by activated STAT3, thereby increasing insulin resistance in mice under 1G conditions [<a href="#B139-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B139-ijms-22-09470">139</a>]. Upregulation of both <em>Tbc1d12</em> and <em>Fbxo40</em> in bedrest and rodent spaceflight models is consistent with reported perturbations in glucose metabolism observed in humans in space [<a href="#B140-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B140-ijms-22-09470">140</a>]. These findings provide a rationale for testing whether targeting <em>Fbxo40</em> and its signaling partners will be useful in mitigating muscle atrophy and altered glucose metabolism in both spaceflight and Earth-based scenarios.</p></section></section><section id="sec4-ijms-22-09470"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">4. Materials and Methods</h2>
<section id="sec4dot1-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.1. Datasets Used in This Study</h3>
<p><a href="#ijms-22-09470-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a> lists the various datasets used in this study. These datasets can be found in NASA GeneLab and are cited in these references [<a href="#B24-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-ijms-22-09470">24</a>,<a href="#B25-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-ijms-22-09470">25</a>,<a href="#B37-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-ijms-22-09470">37</a>,<a href="#B38-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38-ijms-22-09470">38</a>,<a href="#B39-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B39-ijms-22-09470">39</a>,<a href="#B40-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40-ijms-22-09470">40</a>,<a href="#B41-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B41-ijms-22-09470">41</a>]. The datasets involving mice flown on the ISS make use of the NASA Rodent Research notation for the various experimental groups. Specifically, NASA RR-1 validation mice consisted of four groups to also enable assessment of the contributions of age and cage configuration. These include Spaceflight (FLT) and Ground Control (GC) groups, which both make use of the NASA rodent habitat. The GC group was run with a 4-day delay to allow for replication of actual temperature, gas partial pressures, and humidity conditions of the FLT group. In addition, a baseline (Basal) group was euthanized on Earth one day after launch to compare changes in both the FLT and GC groups. Lastly, a Vivarium (Viv) control comprising mice maintained in standard housing was also included to determine the impact of the mouse habitat hardware in the results obtained from the FLT and GC groups. Refer to [<a href="#B17-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-ijms-22-09470">17</a>] for further details. NASA GeneLab generated omics data from the following comparisons: Basal vs Viv controls, GC vs Viv controls, FLT vs GC [<a href="#B17-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-ijms-22-09470">17</a>]. In this work, we focus on the GC and FLT groups. A number of bedrest studies focusing on muscle have been published, with a variety of assay approaches (coding and non-coding RNA), main experimental variables, and tissue collection schemes [<a href="#B24-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-ijms-22-09470">24</a>,<a href="#B141-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B141-ijms-22-09470">141</a>,<a href="#B142-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B142-ijms-22-09470">142</a>]. For this current analysis, we selected a transcriptomic dataset from a previous bedrest study [<a href="#B24-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-ijms-22-09470">24</a>] based on availability of raw expression data from coding RNA.</p></section><section id="sec4dot2-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.2. Processed RNAseq Data</h3>
<p>Differential expression data of RNA-seq datasets (GLDS-99, 103 and 104) were downloaded from NASA GeneLab. The NASA GeneLab online database describes the standard analysis pipeline used to generate this processed data. Briefly, the percentage of rRNA in raw fastq files was assessed using HTStream SeqScreener (version 1.0.0) and then filtered using Trim Galore! (version 0.6.2). The quality of both raw and trimmed reads was evaluated with FastQC [<a href="#B143-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B143-ijms-22-09470">143</a>] (version 0.118), while MultiQC [<a href="#B144-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B144-ijms-22-09470">144</a>] (version 1.7) was used to generate MultiQC reports. Indexes for Mus musculus genome were generated with genome version mm10-GRCm38 (Mus_musculus.GRCm38.dna.toplevel.fa), and Mus_musculus.GRCm38.96.gtf using STAR [<a href="#B145-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B145-ijms-22-09470">145</a>] (version 2.7.1a). Processed reads were aligned to the <em>Mus musculus</em> reference with STAR (version 2.7.1a) and aligned reads were then quantified using RSEM [<a href="#B146-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B146-ijms-22-09470">146</a>] (version 1.3.1). Quantification data was imported to R [<a href="#B147-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B147-ijms-22-09470">147</a>] (version 3.6.0) with tximport [<a href="#B148-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B148-ijms-22-09470">148</a>] (version 1.14.0) and normalized with DESeq2 [<a href="#B149-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B149-ijms-22-09470">149</a>] (version 1.26.0). Differential expression analysis was conducted in R (version 3.6.0) using DESeq2 (version 1.26.0). All groups were compared using the Wald test and the likelihood ratio test was used to generate the F statistic p value. Gene annotations were assigned using the following Bioconductor and annotation packages: STRINGdb [<a href="#B150-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B150-ijms-22-09470">150</a>], PANTHER.db [<a href="#B151-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B151-ijms-22-09470">151</a>], and org.Mm.eg.db [<a href="#B152-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B152-ijms-22-09470">152</a>]. Differential expression analysis using DESeq2 [<a href="#B153-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B153-ijms-22-09470">153</a>] was performed on expression data from space flown subjects against ground controls to assess gene level changes in these muscles and use in downstream pathway analysis.</p></section><section id="sec4dot3-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.3. Processing of Microarray Data</h3>
<p>For Affymetrix microarray datasets (GLDS-3 and GLDS-21), the raw expression data were downloaded from NASA GeneLab database. The data were normalized using the ‘affyNormQC.R’ r script applying the RMA algorithm through the oligo R package with default parameters. The ‘affyNormQC.R’ r script was also used to generate quality control with parameter ‘do.logtranspaceflightorm’ set to TRUE. The microarray experiments were annotated with the r script ‘annotateProbes.R’, which employed Annotation-Db class probe annotations specific to the chip used in each experiment from the Bioconductor repository. In cases where multiple probes mapped to the same gene ID, representative probes were selected with the highest mean normalized intensity across all samples. The limmaDiffExp.R r script was used to perform differential gene expression analysis on normalized expression data to perform pair-wise comparisons for all groups. For each probe set, the variance of mean signal intensities was estimated, improved by an empirical Bayes method for combining variances of probes showing similar variability, and the significance of the difference between the means was evaluated with a <em>t</em>-test to obtain <em>p</em> values. <em>p</em> values were adjusted for multiple hypothesis testing using the Benjamini and Hochberg method to control the false discovery rate [<a href="#B154-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B154-ijms-22-09470">154</a>,<a href="#B155-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B155-ijms-22-09470">155</a>]. Raw Agilent microarray data (GLDS-113 and GLDS-370) were downloaded from NASA GeneLab. Data were analyzed using Gene Spring software (Agilent Technologies, Santa Clara, CA, USA). Background correction was performed using the ‘normexp’ method (with offset = 50), and between array normalization was performed utilizing the quantile normalization method with a log2 transformation. Control probes and those without a RefSeq ID were removed, while probes mapping to the same RefSeq ID were collapsed by mean expression (leaving ~20,700 genes for analysis). All datasets used are outlined in <a href="#ijms-22-09470-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a> and anatomical locations of mammalian muscles are shown in <a href="#ijms-22-09470-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>.</p></section><section id="sec4dot4-ijms-22-09470"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.4. Pathway Analysis</h3>
<p>First, differential expression analysis (DEA) was performed to reveal DEGs, the top 10 of which sorted by fold change values were used to pinpoint central players that orchestrate the response to spaceflight. ORA using WebGestalt [<a href="#B156-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B156-ijms-22-09470">156</a>] was then performed on DEGs to determine enriched gene ontology terms (Log2 FC &gt; 0.32 or &lt; -0.32, FDR adjusted <em>p</em> &lt; 0.05). A variance stabilizing transformation was applied to the count data from datasets GLDS-99 and GLDS-104, and a Gene Set Enrichment Analysis was performed with FDR threshold of &lt; 0.1 using Cytoscape [<a href="#B157-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B157-ijms-22-09470">157</a>]. The EnrichmentMap and Autoannotate Cytoscape plugins were used to visualize and annotate clusters of overlapping gene sets to help identify overarching enriched functional themes and aid in the interpretation of the effects of spaceflight in distinct muscle types. A comparative analysis was performed on the results from each muscle.</p></section></section><section id="sec5-ijms-22-09470"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">5. Conclusions</h2>
<p>This study aimed to determine the effects of spaceflight on the transcriptome of distinct mammalian muscle types and to define shared transcriptomic signatures across a variety of model organisms. Our analyses revealed that spaceflight elicited both shared and discrete responses in representative slow and fast twitch muscle types (soleus and EDL respectively). The shared responses between these muscle types include altered expression of genes involved in GC stress responses and the circadian rhythm. These two muscle types displayed differences in transcriptomic signatures pertaining to immune function and cellular growth, with the EDL exhibiting greater degree of differential regulation of these processes based on the number of differentially expressed genes between spaceflight and ground samples. Additionally, EDL and soleus showed differences in the transcriptomic response of genes involved in ER stress mechanisms. Our findings also highlight the possible role of <em>Pde2a</em> as a key molecule that may confer the atrophy resistance seen in the EDL. We also found that the muscle specific Ubiquitin E ligase <em>Fbxo40</em> was downregulated across models for muscle atrophy (bedrest and spaceflight), suggesting a possible target for countermeasures development. These results were also used as a backdrop for the comparison to spaceflight responses of non-mammalian models. The molecular signature of <em>C. elegans</em> in response to spaceflight showed greater similarity to that of the EDL. Furthermore, the transcriptomic signature of the larval stage of <em>D. melanogaster</em> showed more similarity to the slow twitch muscle soleus. In contrast, the response of adult <em>D. melanogaster</em> was more akin to that of fast twitch muscle EDL. Muscles from rodent spaceflight and human bedrest studies indicate alterations in glucose homeostasis and circadian rhythm which are consistent with findings in humans that experienced spaceflight.</p>
<p>As expected for a highly resource intensive data collection endeavor, spaceflight omics datasets are rare and can have differences in experiment designs. One of the advantages of applying unbiased bioinformatics analyses of gene expression data is that patterns, differences, or similarities in the regulation of biological processes can be observed from a relatively small number of datasets. We have demonstrated that such an approach can be used to continue to gain insight on the shared responses across model organisms and also between ground and flight analogs for microgravity. However, we recognize limitations in this study, including the possibility that differing experiment designs across datasets such as duration of flight, relative life stage and gender and sex can impact the results obtained. In addition, several datasets yielded a relatively small number of differentially expressed genes, which can limit the ability to find shared molecular signatures across multiple datasets. As more spaceflight and analog datasets become available from the various model organisms, follow-up analyses can be conducted using the approach we have employed. We anticipate that doing so will reveal additional shared mechanisms across the model organisms of spaceflight. Although the use of invertebrate models such as <em>D. melanogaster</em> and <em>C. elegans</em> show promise in anticipating select spaceflight responses of specific muscles, our findings also confirm the marked differences in molecular signatures across these model organisms when dissections are not possible. Hence, the use of rodents to extrapolate human responses to spaceflight continue to have major advantages from a physiology perspective versus classic non-mammalian genetic model systems such as <em>D. melanogaster</em> and <em>C. elegans</em>. However, our findings raise the possibility of using invertebrate models as a first step toward conducting precision animal research in future deep space missions.</p></section><section id="ack1" class="ack"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Acknowledgments</h2>
<p>GeneLab data are courtesy of the NASA GeneLab Data Repository [<a href="#B158-ijms-22-09470" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B158-ijms-22-09470">158</a>]. We thank Samrawit Gebre, Matthew Geniza, Homer Fogle, Daniel Berrios, and Amanda Saravia-Butler for guidance and advice on the use of data in the GeneLab database.</p></section><section id="app1-ijms-22-09470" class="app"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Supplementary Materials</h2>

<section id="ref-list1_sec2"><ul class="ref-list font-sm" style="list-style-type:none">
<li id="B1-ijms-22-09470">
